style="margin-left:.25em;">272</span></span>&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcsecond" title="Arcsecond">arcsec</a>&#160;/ <a href="/wiki/Julian_year_(astronomy)" title="Julian year (astronomy)">yr</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">Precession&#160;of the <a href="/wiki/Longitude_of_the_ascending_node" title="Longitude of the ascending node">ascending&#160;node</a></div></th><td><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">2998408299660000000&#9824;</span>&#8722;59.170<span (astronomy)">yr</a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:#D6D6D6"><span id="Infobox_Physical_characteristics"></span><a style="max-width:11em;">Dimensions</th><td><span class="nowrap">(<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002965200000000000&#9824;</span>965.2</span> &#215; <span href="#cite_note-presentation-7">[6]</a></sup></span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">Mean radius</div></th><td><span ascension</a></span></div></th><td>291.42744&#176;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Icarus2017_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Icarus2017-14">[12]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" id="cite_ref-SPICE_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-SPICE-13">[11]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Geometric_albedo" title="Geometric albedo">Geometric albedo</a></div></th><td><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6998900000000000000&#9824;</span>0.090<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>0.0033</span> <span style="font-size:85%;">(V-band)</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li2006_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Li2006-15">[13]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><table style="border-spacing: 0px; width:100%; border:none; background-color:#f9f9f9; margin:0; id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup></td>\n<td>235&#160;K<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saint-Pe1993_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saint-Pe1993-20">[18]</a></sup></td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Asteroid_spectral_types" title="Asteroid spectral types">Spectral type</a></div></th><td><a href="/wiki/C-type_asteroid" title="C-type asteroid">C</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rivkin2006_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rivkin2006-16">[14]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">Apparent magnitude</a></div></th><td>6.64<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pasachoff1983_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pasachoff1983-17">[15]</a></sup> to 9.34<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact3_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-fact3-18">[16]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude#Solar_System_bodies_(H)" title="Absolute magnitude">Absolute magnitude&#160;<i>(H)</i></a></div></th><td><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000334000000000000&#9824;</span>3.34</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpl_sbdb_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-jpl_sbdb-5">[4]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Angular_diameter" title="Angular diameter">Angular diameter</a></div></th><td>0.854&#8243;  to 0.339&#8243;</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n</div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Ceres</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'s\' in \'sigh\'">s</span><span title="/&#618;&#601;r/: \'ear\' in \'near\'">&#618;&#601;r</span><span title="/i&#720;/: \'ee\' in \'fleece\'">i&#720;</span><span title="\'z\' in \'zoom\'">z</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minor-planet_designation" title="Minor-planet designation">minor-planet designation</a>: <b>1 Ceres</b>) is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_notable_asteroids" title="List of notable asteroids">largest object</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a> that lies between the orbits of <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, slightly closer to <a href="/wiki/Orbit_of_Mars" title="Orbit of Mars">Mars\'s orbit</a>. With a diameter of 945&#160;km (587&#160;mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-presentation_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-presentation-7">[6]</a></sup> Ceres is the largest of the <a href="/wiki/Minor_planet" title="Minor planet">minor planets</a> and the only <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a> inside <a href="/wiki/Cis-Neptunian_object" title="Cis-Neptunian object">Neptune\'s orbit</a>. It is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Solar_System_objects_by_size" title="List of Solar System objects by size">33rd-largest known body</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[c]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PBE-20150215_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-PBE-20150215-23">[20]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ceres is composed of rock and ice and is estimated to comprise approximately one-third of the mass of the entire asteroid belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-24">[21]</a></sup> Ceres is the only object in the asteroid belt known to be <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">rounded by its own gravity</a> (though detailed analysis was required to exclude <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">Vesta</a>). From <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> of Ceres ranges from 6.7 to 9.3, peaking once in <a href="/wiki/Opposition_(planets)" title="Opposition (planets)">opposition</a> every 15 to 16 months<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[22]</a></sup> (its synodic period); thus even at its brightest, it appears too dim to be seen by the <a href="/wiki/Naked_eye" title="Naked eye">naked eye</a>, except under extremely dark skies.\n</p><p>Ceres was the first asteroid to be discovered (by <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Piazzi" title="Giuseppe Piazzi">Giuseppe Piazzi</a> at <a href="/wiki/Palermo_Astronomical_Observatory" title="Palermo Astronomical Observatory">Palermo Astronomical Observatory</a> on 1 January 1801).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[23]</a></sup> It was originally considered a planet, but was reclassified as an asteroid in the 1850s after many other objects in similar orbits were discovered.\n</p><p>Ceres appears to be <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">differentiated</a> into a <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rocky</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core_(geology)" title="Core (geology)">core</a> and an icy <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a>, and may have a remnant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internal_ocean" title="Internal ocean">internal ocean</a> of <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_liquid_water" title="Extraterrestrial liquid water">liquid water</a> under the layer of ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCord2005-jgrp_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCord2005-jgrp-27">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O\'Brien2015_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-O\'Brien2015-28">[25]</a></sup> The surface is a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">water ice</a> and various <a href="/wiki/Hydrate" title="Hydrate">hydrated</a> minerals such as <a href="/wiki/Carbonate_minerals" title="Carbonate minerals">carbonates</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clay_mineral" title="Clay mineral">clay</a>. In January 2014, emissions of water vapor were detected from several regions of Ceres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup> This was unexpected because large bodies in the asteroid belt typically do not emit vapor, a hallmark of comets.\n</p><p>The robotic NASA spacecraft <a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)"><i>Dawn</i></a> entered orbit around Ceres on 6 March 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150306_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150306-30">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arrival-jpl.nasa.gov_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-arrival-jpl.nasa.gov-31">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rayman2015.03.06_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rayman2015.03.06-32">[29]</a></sup> Pictures with a resolution previously unattained were taken during imaging sessions starting in January 2015 as <i>Dawn</i> approached Ceres, showing a cratered surface. Two distinct <a href="/wiki/Bright_spots_on_Ceres" title="Bright spots on Ceres">bright spots</a> (or high-<a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> features) inside a crater (different from the bright spots observed in earlier <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble</a> images<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SL-20150511_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-SL-20150511-33">[30]</a></sup>) were seen in a 19 February 2015 image, leading to speculation about a possible <a href="/wiki/Cryovolcano" title="Cryovolcano">cryovolcanic</a> origin<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O\'Neill2015.02.25_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-O\'Neill2015.02.25-34">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landau2015.02.25_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landau2015.02.25-35">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla2015.02.26_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla2015.02.26-36">[33]</a></sup> or outgassing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planetary.org_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-planetary.org-37">[34]</a></sup> On 3 March 2015, a NASA spokesperson said the spots are consistent with highly reflective materials containing ice or salts, but that cryovolcanism is unlikely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UT-20150303_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-UT-20150303-38">[35]</a></sup> However, on 2 September 2016, scientists from the Dawn team claimed in a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science_(magazine)" title="Science (magazine)">Science</a></i> paper that a massive cryovolcano called <a href="/wiki/Ahuna_Mons" title="Ahuna Mons">Ahuna Mons</a> is the strongest evidence yet for the existence of these mysterious formations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup> On 11 May 2015, NASA released a higher-resolution image showing that, instead of one or two spots, there are actually several.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[38]</a></sup> On 9 December 2015, NASA scientists reported that the bright spots on Ceres may be related to a type of salt, particularly a form of <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a> containing <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_sulfate" title="Magnesium sulfate">magnesium sulfate</a> <a href="/wiki/Sulfate_minerals" title="Sulfate minerals">hexahydrite</a> (MgSO<sub>4</sub>&#183;6H<sub>2</sub>O); the spots were also found to be associated with <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>-rich <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clays.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20151209-el_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20151209-el-42">[39]</a></sup> In June 2016, near-infrared spectra of these bright areas were found to be consistent with a large amount of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_carbonate" title="Sodium carbonate">sodium carbonate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>), implying that recent geologic activity was probably involved in the creation of the bright spots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20160629_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20160629-43">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewin_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lewin-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-De_Sanctis_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-De_Sanctis-45">[42]</a></sup> In July 2018, NASA released a comparison of physical features found on Ceres with similar ones present on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20180724_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20180724-46">[43]</a></sup> From June to October, 2018, <i>Dawn</i> orbited Ceres from as close as 35&#160;km (22&#160;mi) and as far away as 4,000&#160;km (2,500&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20180613_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20180613-47">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFN-20180615_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-SFN-20180615-48">[45]</a></sup> The <i>Dawn</i> mission ended on 1 November 2018 after the spacecraft ran out of fuel.\n</p><p>In October 2015, NASA released a true-color portrait of Ceres made by <i>Dawn</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup> In February 2017, <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organics</a> (<a href="/wiki/Tholin" title="Tholin">tholins</a>) were  detected on Ceres in Ernutet crater (<a href="/wiki/File:PIA21419-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-ErnutetCrater-OrganicsDetected-20170216.jpg" title="File:PIA21419-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-ErnutetCrater-OrganicsDetected-20170216.jpg">see image</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PO-20170216_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-PO-20170216-50">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-organics_2018_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-organics_2018-51">[48]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Discovery"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Discovery</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Name"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Name</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Classification"><span class="tocnumber">1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Classification</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Orbit"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Orbit</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Resonances"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Resonances</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Transits_of_planets_from_Ceres"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Transits of planets from Ceres</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Rotation_and_axial_tilt"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Rotation and axial tilt</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Geology"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Geology</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Surface"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Surface</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-11"><a href="#Observations_prior_to_Dawn"><span class="tocnumber">4.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Observations prior to <i>Dawn</i></span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-12"><a href="#Observations_by_Dawn"><span class="tocnumber">4.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Observations by <i>Dawn</i></span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-4 tocsection-13"><a href="#Carbon"><span class="tocnumber">4.1.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Carbon</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Internal_structure"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Internal structure</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Atmosphere"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Atmosphere</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Origin_and_evolution"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Origin and evolution</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Potential_habitability"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Potential habitability</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Observation_and_exploration"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Observation and exploration</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Observation"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">Observation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Exploration"><span class="tocnumber">8.2</span> <span class="toctext">Exploration</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Maps"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Maps</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Map_of_quadrangles"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Map of quadrangles</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Gallery"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Gallery</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#True-color_images"><span class="tocnumber">10.1</span> <span class="toctext">True-color images</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Animations"><span class="tocnumber">10.2</span> <span class="toctext">Animations</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cerere_Ferdinandea.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="678" data-file-width="442" decoding="async" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Cerere_Ferdinandea.gif/170px-Cerere_Ferdinandea.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Cerere_Ferdinandea.gif/255px-Cerere_Ferdinandea.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Cerere_Ferdinandea.gif/340px-Cerere_Ferdinandea.gif 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cerere_Ferdinandea.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Piazzi\'s book <i>Della scoperta del nuovo pianeta Cerere Ferdinandea</i>, outlining the discovery of Ceres, dedicated the new "planet" to <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_I_of_the_Two_Sicilies" title="Ferdinand I of the Two Sicilies">Ferdinand I of the Two Sicilies</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Johann_Elert_Bode" title="Johann Elert Bode">Johann Elert Bode</a>, in 1772, first suggested that an undiscovered planet could exist between the orbits of <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hoskin_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-hoskin-52">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Kepler</a> had already noticed the gap between Mars and Jupiter in 1596.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hoskin_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-hoskin-52">[49]</a></sup> Bode based his idea on the <a href="/wiki/Titius%E2%80%93Bode_law" title="Titius&#8211;Bode law">Titius&#8211;Bode law</a> which is a now-discredited hypothesis that was first proposed in 1766.  Bode observed that there was a regular pattern in the semi-major axes of the orbits of known planets, and that the pattern was marred only by the large gap between Mars and Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hoskin_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-hoskin-52">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hogg1948_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hogg1948-53">[50]</a></sup> The pattern predicted that the missing planet ought to have an orbit with a semi-major axis near 2.8 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical units</a> (AU).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hogg1948_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hogg1948-53">[50]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a>\'s discovery of <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> in 1781<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hoskin_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-hoskin-52">[49]</a></sup> near the predicted distance for the next body beyond <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> increased faith in the law of Titius and Bode, and in 1800, a group headed by <a href="/wiki/Franz_Xaver_von_Zach" title="Franz Xaver von Zach">Franz Xaver von Zach</a>, editor of the <i>Monatliche Correspondenz</i>, sent requests to twenty-four experienced astronomers (whom he dubbed the "celestial police"), asking that they combine their efforts and begin a methodical search for the expected planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hoskin_52-4"><a href="#cite_note-hoskin-52">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hogg1948_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hogg1948-53">[50]</a></sup> Although they did not discover Ceres, they later found several large <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hogg1948_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hogg1948-53">[50]</a></sup>\n</p><p>One of the astronomers selected for the search was <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Piazzi" title="Giuseppe Piazzi">Giuseppe Piazzi</a>, a Catholic priest at the Academy of <a href="/wiki/Palermo" title="Palermo">Palermo</a>, Sicily. Before receiving his invitation to join the group, Piazzi discovered Ceres on 1 January 1801.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20160126_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20160126-55">[52]</a></sup> He was searching for "the 87th [star] of the Catalogue of the Zodiacal stars of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicolas_Louis_de_Lacaille" title="Nicolas Louis de Lacaille">Mr la Caille</a>", but found that "it was preceded by another".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hoskin_52-5"><a href="#cite_note-hoskin-52">[49]</a></sup> Instead of a star, Piazzi had found a moving star-like object, which he first thought was a <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forbes1971_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes1971-56">[53]</a></sup> Piazzi observed Ceres a total of 24 times, the final time on 11 February 1801, when illness interrupted his observations. He announced his discovery on 24 January 1801 in letters to only two fellow astronomers, his compatriot <a href="/wiki/Barnaba_Oriani" title="Barnaba Oriani">Barnaba Oriani</a> of <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Elert_Bode" title="Johann Elert Bode">Johann Elert Bode</a> of <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup> He reported it as a comet but "since its movement is so slow and rather uniform, it has occurred to me several times that it might be something better than a comet".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hoskin_52-6"><a href="#cite_note-hoskin-52">[49]</a></sup> In April, Piazzi sent his complete observations to Oriani, Bode, and <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_Lalande" title="J&#233;r&#244;me Lalande">J&#233;r&#244;me Lalande</a> in Paris. The information was published in the September 1801 issue of the <i>Monatliche Correspondenz</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forbes1971_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes1971-56">[53]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By this time, the apparent position of Ceres had changed (mostly due to Earth\'s orbital motion), and was too close to the Sun\'s glare for other astronomers to confirm Piazzi\'s observations. Toward the end of the year, Ceres should have been visible again, but after such a long time it was difficult to predict its exact position. To recover Ceres, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Carl Friedrich Gauss</a>, then 24 years old, developed an <a href="/wiki/Gauss%27s_method" title="Gauss\'s method">efficient method</a> of <a href="/wiki/Orbit_determination" title="Orbit determination">orbit determination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forbes1971_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes1971-56">[53]</a></sup> In only a few weeks, he predicted the path of Ceres and sent his results to von Zach. On 31 December 1801, von Zach and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heinrich_Wilhelm_Matth%C3%A4us_Olbers" title="Heinrich Wilhelm Matth&#228;us Olbers">Heinrich W. M. Olbers</a> found Ceres near the predicted position and thus recovered it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forbes1971_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes1971-56">[53]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The early observers were only able to calculate the size of Ceres to within an <a href="/wiki/Order_of_magnitude" title="Order of magnitude">order of magnitude</a>. Herschel underestimated its diameter as 260&#160;km in 1802, whereas in 1811 <a href="/wiki/Johann_Hieronymus_Schr%C3%B6ter" title="Johann Hieronymus Schr&#246;ter">Johann Hieronymus Schr&#246;ter</a> overestimated it as 2,613&#160;km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes1994_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes1994-59">[56]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Piazzi originally suggested the name <i>Cerere Ferdinandea</i> for his discovery, after the goddess <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceres_(Roman_mythology)" title="Ceres (Roman mythology)">Ceres</a> (<a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman goddess</a> of agriculture, <i>Cerere</i> in Italian, who was believed to have originated in Sicily and whose oldest temple was there) and <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_I_of_the_Two_Sicilies" title="Ferdinand I of the Two Sicilies">King Ferdinand</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sicily" title="Kingdom of Sicily">Sicily</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hoskin_52-7"><a href="#cite_note-hoskin-52">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forbes1971_56-4"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes1971-56">[53]</a></sup> "Ferdinandea", however, was not acceptable to other nations and was dropped.\nCeres was called <i><a href="/wiki/Hera" title="Hera">Hera</a></i> for a short time in Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[57]</a></sup>\nIn Greece, it is called <a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a> (&#916;&#942;&#956;&#951;&#964;&#961;&#945;), after the Greek equivalent of the Roman <i>Cer&#275;s</i>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[d]</a></sup> in English, that name is used for the asteroid <a href="/wiki/1108_Demeter" title="1108 Demeter">1108 Demeter</a>.\n</p><p>The regular adjectival forms of the name are <i>Cererian</i> and <i>Cererean</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[58]</a></sup> derived from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genitive" title="Genitive">genitive</a> <i>Cereris</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simpson1979_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Simpson1979-3">[2]</a></sup> but <i>Ceresian</i> is occasionally seen for the goddess (as in the sickle-shaped <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceresian_Lake" title="Ceresian Lake">Ceresian Lake</a>), as is the shorter form <i>Cerean</i>.\n</p><p>The old <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_symbols" title="Astronomical symbols">astronomical symbol</a> of Ceres is a <a href="/wiki/Sickle" title="Sickle">sickle</a>, &#10216;&#9907;&#10217; (<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ceres_symbol.svg" title="Sickle variant symbol of Ceres"><img alt="Sickle variant symbol of Ceres" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="50" decoding="async" height="18" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Ceres_symbol.svg/18px-Ceres_symbol.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Ceres_symbol.svg/27px-Ceres_symbol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Ceres_symbol.svg/36px-Ceres_symbol.svg.png 2x" width="18"/></a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[59]</a></sup> similar to <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>\' symbol &#10216;<span style="line-height:170%"><span style="font-size:170%;">&#9792;</span></span>&#10217; but with a break in the circle.  It has a variant &#10216; <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ceres2.svg" title="Cee variant symbol of Ceres"><img alt="Cee variant symbol of Ceres" data-file-height="492" data-file-width="249" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Ceres2.svg/8px-Ceres2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Ceres2.svg/12px-Ceres2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Ceres2.svg/16px-Ceres2.svg.png 2x" width="8"/></a> &#10217;, reversed under the influence of the initial letter \'C\' of \'Ceres\'.  These were later replaced with the generic asteroid symbol of a numbered disk, &#10216;&#9312;&#10217;.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forbes1971_56-5"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes1971-56">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[60]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cerium" title="Cerium">Cerium</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Rare-earth_element" title="Rare-earth element">rare-earth element</a> discovered in 1803, was named after Ceres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[e]</a></sup> In the same year another element was also initially named after Ceres, but when cerium was named, its discoverer changed the name to <a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">palladium</a>, after the second asteroid, <a href="/wiki/2_Pallas" title="2 Pallas">2 Pallas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[63]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The categorization of Ceres has changed more than once and has been the subject of some disagreement. Johann Elert Bode believed Ceres to be the "missing planet" he had proposed to exist between Mars and Jupiter, at a distance of 419&#160;million&#160;km (2.8&#160;AU) from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hoskin_52-8"><a href="#cite_note-hoskin-52">[49]</a></sup> Ceres was assigned a planetary symbol, and <a href="/wiki/Planet#Objects_formerly_considered_planets" title="Planet">remained listed as a planet</a> in astronomy books and tables (along with <a href="/wiki/2_Pallas" title="2 Pallas">2 Pallas</a>, <a href="/wiki/3_Juno" title="3 Juno">3 Juno</a>, and <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">4 Vesta</a>) for half a century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hoskin_52-9"><a href="#cite_note-hoskin-52">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forbes1971_56-6"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes1971-56">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hilton_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hilton-69">[64]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moon_and_Asteroids_1_to_10.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Moon_and_Asteroids_1_to_10.svg/220px-Moon_and_Asteroids_1_to_10.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Moon_and_Asteroids_1_to_10.svg/330px-Moon_and_Asteroids_1_to_10.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Moon_and_Asteroids_1_to_10.svg/440px-Moon_and_Asteroids_1_to_10.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moon_and_Asteroids_1_to_10.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sizes of the first ten main-belt objects discovered profiled against the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. Ceres is far left (1).</div></div></div>\n<p>As other objects were discovered in the neighborhood of Ceres, it was realized that Ceres represented the first of a new class of objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hoskin_52-10"><a href="#cite_note-hoskin-52">[49]</a></sup> In 1802, with the discovery of 2 Pallas, William Herschel coined the term <i>asteroid</i> ("star-like") for these bodies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hilton_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hilton-69">[64]</a></sup> writing that "they resemble small stars so much as hardly to be distinguished from them, even by very good telescopes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[65]</a></sup> As the first such body to be discovered, Ceres was given the designation 1 Ceres under the modern system of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minor-planet_designation" title="Minor-planet designation">minor-planet designations</a>. By the 1860s, the existence of a fundamental difference between asteroids such as Ceres and the major planets was widely accepted, though a precise definition of "planet" was never formulated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hilton_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hilton-69">[64]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:154px;max-width:154px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ceres,_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg"><img alt="Ceres (bottom left), the Moon and Earth, shown to scale" data-file-height="2026" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="101" style="clear:left">Ceres (bottom left), the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> and Earth, shown to scale</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Eros%2C_Vesta_and_Ceres_size_comparison.jpg/225px-Eros%2C_Vesta_and_Ceres_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Eros%2C_Vesta_and_Ceres_size_comparison.jpg/300px-Eros%2C_Vesta_and_Ceres_size_comparison.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Size comparison of <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">Vesta</a>, Ceres and <a href="/wiki/433_Eros" title="433 Eros">Eros</a></div></div></div></div>\n<p>The 2006 debate surrounding <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> and what constitutes a planet led to Ceres being considered for reclassification as a planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[67]</a></sup> A proposal before the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Definition_of_planet" title="Definition of planet">definition of a planet</a> would have defined a planet as "a celestial body that (a) has sufficient mass for its self-gravity to overcome rigid-body forces so that it assumes a hydrostatic equilibrium (nearly round) shape, and (b) is in orbit around a star, and is neither a star nor a satellite of a planet".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[68]</a></sup> Had this resolution been adopted, it would have made Ceres the fifth planet in order from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[69]</a></sup> This never happened, however, and on 24 August 2006 a modified definition was adopted, carrying the additional requirement that a planet must have "<a href="/wiki/Clearing_the_neighbourhood" title="Clearing the neighbourhood">cleared the neighborhood</a> around its orbit". By this definition, Ceres is not a planet because it does not dominate its orbit, sharing it as it does with the thousands of other asteroids in the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a> and constituting only about a third of the mass of the belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-24">[21]</a></sup> Bodies that met the first proposed definition but not the second, such as Ceres, were instead classified as <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planets</a>.\n</p><p>Ceres is the largest object in the asteroid belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rivkin2006_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rivkin2006-16">[14]</a></sup>\nIt is sometimes assumed that Ceres has been <i>re</i>classified as a dwarf planet, and that it is therefore no longer considered an asteroid. For example, a news update at Space.com spoke of "Pallas, the largest asteroid, and Ceres, the dwarf planet formerly classified as an asteroid",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[70]</a></sup> whereas an IAU question-and-answer posting states, "Ceres is (or now we can say it was) the largest asteroid", though it then speaks of "other asteroids" crossing Ceres\' path and otherwise implies that Ceres is still considered an asteroid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU-QA_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU-QA-76">[71]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Minor_Planet_Center" title="Minor Planet Center">Minor Planet Center</a> notes that such bodies may have dual designations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[72]</a></sup> The 2006 IAU decision that classified Ceres as a dwarf planet never addressed whether it is or is not an asteroid. Indeed, the IAU has never defined the word \'asteroid\' at all, having preferred the term \'<a href="/wiki/Minor_planet" title="Minor planet">minor planet</a>\' until 2006, and preferring the terms \'<a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">small Solar System body</a>\' and \'dwarf planet\' after 2006. Lang (2011) comments "the [IAU has] added a new designation to Ceres, classifying it as a dwarf planet.&#160;... By [its] definition, <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a> and Pluto, as well as the largest asteroid, 1 Ceres, are all dwarf planets", and describes it elsewhere as "the dwarf planet&#8211;asteroid 1 Ceres".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[73]</a></sup> NASA continues to refer to Ceres as an asteroid,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[74]</a></sup> as do various academic textbooks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[76]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit">Orbit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Orbit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div style="float:right; margin:8px;">\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">\n<caption>Proper (long-term mean) orbital elements compared to osculating (instant) orbital elements for Ceres:\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Element<br/>type</th>\n<th><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">a</a><br/>(in <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a>)</th>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">e</a></th>\n<th><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_Inclination" title="Orbital Inclination">i</a></th>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">Period</a><br/>(in days)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Proper_orbital_elements" title="Proper orbital elements">Proper</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ceres-POE_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ceres-POE-6">[5]</a></sup></td>\n<td>2.7671</td>\n<td>0.116198</td>\n<td>9.647435</td>\n<td>1,681.60\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Osculating_orbit" title="Osculating orbit">Osculating</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpl_sbdb_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-jpl_sbdb-5">[4]</a></sup><br/>(<a href="/wiki/Epoch_(astronomy)" title="Epoch (astronomy)">Epoch</a> 23 July 2010 )</td>\n<td>2.7653</td>\n<td>0.079138</td>\n<td>10.586821</td>\n<td>1,679.66\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Difference</td>\n<td>0.0018</td>\n<td>0.03706</td>\n<td>0.939386</td>\n<td>1.94\n</td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ceres_Orbit.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="563" data-file-width="742" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Ceres_Orbit.svg/250px-Ceres_Orbit.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Ceres_Orbit.svg/375px-Ceres_Orbit.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Ceres_Orbit.svg/500px-Ceres_Orbit.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ceres_Orbit.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Orbit of Ceres</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif/250px-Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif/375px-Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif/500px-Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Dawn_trajectory.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of <i> Dawn</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> trajectory from 27 September 2007 to 5 October 2018<br/><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:magenta; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160; <i><a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)">Dawn</a></i> </span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Royalblue; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Gold; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Cyan; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">4 Vesta</a> </span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:lime; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;1 Ceres</span></div></div></div>\n<p>Ceres follows an orbit between Mars and Jupiter, within the asteroid belt, with a period of 4.6 Earth years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpl_sbdb_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-jpl_sbdb-5">[4]</a></sup> The orbit is moderately inclined (<i>i</i> = 10.6&#176; compared to 7&#176; for <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> and 17&#176; for Pluto) and moderately <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric</a> (<i>e</i> = 0.08 compared to 0.09 for Mars).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpl_sbdb_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-jpl_sbdb-5">[4]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The diagram illustrates the orbits of Ceres (blue) and several planets (white and gray). The segments of orbits below the ecliptic are plotted in darker colors, and the orange plus sign is the Sun\'s location. The top left diagram is a polar view that shows the location of Ceres in the gap between Mars and Jupiter. The top right is a close-up demonstrating the locations of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pericenter" title="Pericenter">perihelia</a> (q) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apocenter" title="Apocenter">aphelia</a> (Q) of Ceres and Mars. In this diagram (but not in general), the perihelion of Mars is on the opposite side of the Sun from those of Ceres and several of the large main-belt asteroids, including 2 Pallas and <a href="/wiki/10_Hygiea" title="10 Hygiea">10 Hygiea</a>. The bottom diagram is a side view showing the inclination of the orbit of Ceres compared to the orbits of Mars and Jupiter.\n</p><p>Ceres was once thought to be a member of an <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_family" title="Asteroid family">asteroid family</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellino_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cellino-82">[77]</a></sup> The asteroids of this family share similar <a href="/wiki/Proper_orbital_elements" title="Proper orbital elements">proper orbital elements</a>, which may indicate a common origin through an asteroid collision some time in the past. Ceres was later found to have spectral properties different from other members of the family, which is now called the <a href="/wiki/Gefion_family" title="Gefion family">Gefion family</a> after the next-lowest-numbered family member, <a href="/wiki/1272_Gefion" title="1272 Gefion">1272 Gefion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cellino_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cellino-82">[77]</a></sup> Ceres appears to be merely an interloper in the Gefion family, coincidentally having similar orbital elements but not a common origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelley_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelley-83">[78]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resonances">Resonances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Resonances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Ceres is in a <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance#Coincidental_\'near\'_ratios_of_mean_motion" title="Orbital resonance">near</a>-1:1 mean-motion <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonance</a> with Pallas (their proper orbital periods differ by 0.2%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kova&#269;evi&#263;_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kova&#269;evi&#263;-84">[79]</a></sup> However, a true resonance between the two would be unlikely; due to their small masses relative to their large separations, such relationships among asteroids are very rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christou_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christou-85">[80]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Ceres is able to capture other asteroids into temporary 1:1 resonant orbital relationships (making them temporary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trojan_(astronomy)" title="Trojan (astronomy)">trojans</a>) for periods up to 2 million years or more; fifty such objects have been identified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christou2012_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christou2012-86">[81]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transits_of_planets_from_Ceres">Transits of planets from Ceres</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Transits of planets from Ceres">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Mercury, Venus, Earth, and Mars can all appear to cross the Sun, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_transit" title="Astronomical transit">transit</a> it, from a vantage point on Ceres. The most common transits are those of Mercury, which usually happen every few years, most recently in 2006 and 2010. The most recent transit of Venus was in 1953, and the next will be in 2051; the corresponding dates are 1814 and 2081 for transits of Earth, and 767 and 2684 for transits of Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[82]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rotation_and_axial_tilt">Rotation and axial tilt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Rotation and axial tilt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The rotation period of Ceres (the Cererian day) is 9 hours and 4&#160;minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDC_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDC-88">[83]</a></sup> It has an axial tilt of 4&#176;. This is small enough for Ceres\'s polar regions to contain permanently shadowed craters that are expected to act as <a href="/wiki/Cold_trap" title="Cold trap">cold traps</a> and accumulate water ice over time, similar to the situation on the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_water#Trapping" title="Lunar water">Moon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)#Surface_conditions_and_exosphere" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>. About 0.14% of water molecules released from the surface are expected to end up in the traps, hopping an average of 3 times before escaping or being trapped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schorghofer2016_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schorghofer2016-89">[84]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Ceres" title="Geology of Ceres">Geology of Ceres</a></div>\n<p>Ceres has a mass of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7020939000000000000&#9824;</span>9.39<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>20</sup>&#160;kg</span> as determined from the <i>Dawn</i> spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rayman20150528_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rayman20150528-90">[85]</a></sup> With this mass Ceres composes approximately a third of the estimated total 3.0&#160;&#177;&#160;0.2<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">21</span></sup>&#160;kg mass of the asteroid belt,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pitjeva2005_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pitjeva2005-91">[86]</a></sup> which is in turn approximately 4% of the mass of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. Ceres is massive enough to give it a nearly spherical, <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">equilibrium</a> shape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2005_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas2005-92">[87]</a></sup> Among Solar System bodies, Ceres is intermediate in size between the smaller Vesta and the larger <a href="/wiki/Tethys_(moon)" title="Tethys (moon)">Tethys</a>. Its surface area is approximately the same as the land area of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> or <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[88]</a></sup> In July 2018, NASA released a comparison of physical features found on Ceres with similar ones present on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20180724_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20180724-46">[43]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surface">Surface</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Surface">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_geological_features_on_Ceres" title="List of geological features on Ceres">List of geological features on Ceres</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA22090-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-NotableFeatures-20180117.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="904" data-file-width="1214" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/PIA22090-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-NotableFeatures-20180117.jpg/250px-PIA22090-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-NotableFeatures-20180117.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/PIA22090-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-NotableFeatures-20180117.jpg/375px-PIA22090-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-NotableFeatures-20180117.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/PIA22090-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-NotableFeatures-20180117.jpg/500px-PIA22090-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-NotableFeatures-20180117.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA22090-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-NotableFeatures-20180117.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Notable geological features on Ceres</div></div></div>\n<p>The surface composition of Ceres is broadly similar to that of <a href="/wiki/C-type_asteroid" title="C-type asteroid">C-type asteroids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rivkin2006_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rivkin2006-16">[14]</a></sup> Some differences do exist. The ubiquitous features in Ceres\' <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">IR</a> <a href="/wiki/Spectrum" title="Spectrum">spectrum</a> are those of hydrated materials, which indicate the presence of significant amounts of water in its interior. Other possible surface constituents include iron-rich clay minerals (<a href="/wiki/Cronstedtite" title="Cronstedtite">cronstedtite</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Carbonate_minerals" title="Carbonate minerals">carbonate minerals</a> (<a href="/wiki/Dolomite" title="Dolomite">dolomite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siderite" title="Siderite">siderite</a>), which are common minerals in <a href="/wiki/Carbonaceous_chondrite" title="Carbonaceous chondrite">carbonaceous chondrite</a> meteorites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rivkin2006_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Rivkin2006-16">[14]</a></sup> The spectral features of carbonates and clay minerals are usually absent in the spectra of other C-type asteroids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rivkin2006_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-Rivkin2006-16">[14]</a></sup> Sometimes Ceres is classified as a <a href="/wiki/G-type_asteroid" title="G-type asteroid">G-type asteroid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parker2002_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parker2002-94">[89]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ceres\' surface is relatively warm. The maximum temperature with the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> overhead was estimated from measurements to be 235&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a> (approximately &#8722;38&#160;&#176;C, &#8722;36&#160;&#176;F) on 5 May 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saint-Pe1993_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Saint-Pe1993-20">[18]</a></sup>  Ice is unstable at this temperature.  Material left behind by the sublimation of surface ice could explain the dark surface of Ceres compared to the icy moons of the outer Solar System.\n</p><p>Studies by the Hubble Space Telescope reveal that graphite, sulfur, and sulfur dioxide are present on Ceres\'s surface. The former is evidently the result of space weathering on Ceres\'s older surfaces; the latter two are volatile under Cererian conditions and would be expected to either escape quickly or settle in cold traps, and are evidently associated with areas with recent geological activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[90]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Observations_prior_to_Dawn">Observations prior to <i>Dawn</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Observations prior to Dawn">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ceres_Rotation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="781" data-file-width="880" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Ceres_Rotation.jpg/150px-Ceres_Rotation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Ceres_Rotation.jpg/225px-Ceres_Rotation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Ceres_Rotation.jpg/300px-Ceres_Rotation.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ceres_Rotation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">HST</a></i> images taken over a span of 2 hours and 20 minutes in 2004</div></div></div>\n<p>Prior to the <i>Dawn</i> mission, only a few surface features had been unambiguously detected on Ceres. High-resolution <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a></i> images taken in 1995 showed a dark spot on its surface, which was nicknamed "Piazzi" in honor of the discoverer of Ceres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parker2002_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Parker2002-94">[89]</a></sup> This was thought to be a crater. Later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-infrared" title="Near-infrared">near-infrared</a> images with a higher resolution taken over a whole rotation with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keck_telescope" title="Keck telescope">Keck telescope</a> using <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_optics" title="Adaptive optics">adaptive optics</a> showed several bright and dark features moving with Ceres\' rotation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carry2008_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carry2008-96">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keck_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keck-97">[92]</a></sup> Two dark features had circular shapes and were presumed to be craters; one of them was observed to have a bright central region, whereas another was identified as the "Piazzi" feature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carry2008_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carry2008-96">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keck_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-Keck-97">[92]</a></sup> Visible-light <i>Hubble Space Telescope</i> images of a full rotation taken in 2003 and 2004 showed eleven recognizable surface features, the natures of which were then undetermined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li2006_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Li2006-15">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hubbl12003-4_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hubbl12003-4-98">[93]</a></sup> One of these features corresponds to the "Piazzi" feature observed earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li2006_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Li2006-15">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>These last observations indicated that the north pole of Ceres pointed in the direction of <a href="/wiki/Right_ascension" title="Right ascension">right ascension</a> 19&#160;h 24&#160;min (291&#176;), <a href="/wiki/Declination" title="Declination">declination</a> +59&#176;, in the <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellation</a> <a href="/wiki/Draco_(constellation)" title="Draco (constellation)">Draco</a>, resulting in an <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">axial tilt</a> of approximately 3&#176;.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li2006_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Li2006-15">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2005_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas2005-92">[87]</a></sup> <i>Dawn</i> later determined that  the north polar axis actually points at right ascension 19&#160;h 25&#160;m 40.3&#160;s (291.418&#176;), declination +66&#176; 45\' 50" (about 1.5 degrees from <a href="/wiki/Delta_Draconis" title="Delta Draconis">Delta Draconis</a>), which means an axial tilt of 4&#176;.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-presentation_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-presentation-7">[6]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Observations_by_Dawn">Observations by <i>Dawn</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Observations by Dawn">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload=\'&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Permanent_Shadows_on_Ceres.webm/854px--Permanent_Shadows_on_Ceres.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="32.666" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Permanent_Shadows_on_Ceres.webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/4f/Permanent_Shadows_on_Ceres.webm/Permanent_Shadows_on_Ceres.webm.480p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;" data-title="HD VP9 (720P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 720P" data-transcodekey="720p.vp9.webm" data-width="1280" data-height="720" data-bandwidth="2310296" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/4f/Permanent_Shadows_on_Ceres.webm/Permanent_Shadows_on_Ceres.webm.120p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth VP9 (180P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 180P" data-transcodekey="180p.vp9.webm" data-width="320" data-height="180" data-bandwidth="291904" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/4f/Permanent_Shadows_on_Ceres.webm/Permanent_Shadows_on_Ceres.webm.240p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="314352" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/4f/Permanent_Shadows_on_Ceres.webm/Permanent_Shadows_on_Ceres.webm.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;" data-title="Small VP9 (240P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="405376" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/4f/Permanent_Shadows_on_Ceres.webm/Permanent_Shadows_on_Ceres.webm.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" data-bandwidth="566112" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/4f/Permanent_Shadows_on_Ceres.webm/Permanent_Shadows_on_Ceres.webm.360p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;" data-title="VP9 (360P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 360P" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" data-bandwidth="722520" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;\'><img alt="File:Permanent Shadows on Ceres.webm" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Permanent_Shadows_on_Ceres.webm/220px--Permanent_Shadows_on_Ceres.webm.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4f/Permanent_Shadows_on_Ceres.webm" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Permanent_Shadows_on_Ceres.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Permanently shadowed regions capable of accumulating surface ice were identified in the northern hemisphere of Ceres using <i>Dawn</i>.</div></div></div>\n<p><i>Dawn</i> revealed that Ceres has a heavily cratered surface; nevertheless, Ceres does not have as many large craters as expected, likely due to past geological processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[95]</a></sup> An unexpectedly large number of Cererian craters have central pits, perhaps due to cryovolcanic processes, and many have central peaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[96]</a></sup> Ceres has one prominent mountain, Ahuna Mons; this peak appears to be a cryovolcano and has few craters, suggesting a maximum age of no more than a few hundred million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[98]</a></sup> A later <a href="/wiki/Computer_simulation" title="Computer simulation">computer simulation</a> has suggested that there were originally other cryovolcanoes on Ceres that are now unrecognisable due to <a href="/wiki/Viscoelasticity" title="Viscoelasticity">viscous relaxation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanishing_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-vanishing-104">[99]</a></sup> Several <a href="/wiki/Bright_spots_on_Ceres" title="Bright spots on Ceres">bright spots</a> have been observed by <i>Dawn</i>, the brightest spot ("Spot 5") located in the middle of an 80-kilometer (50&#160;mi) crater called <a href="/wiki/Occator_(crater)" title="Occator (crater)">Occator</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[100]</a></sup> From images taken of Ceres on 4 May 2015, the secondary bright spot was revealed to actually be a group of scattered bright areas, possibly as many as ten. These bright features have an albedo of approximately 40%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siliconvalleyastrolecture_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-siliconvalleyastrolecture-106">[101]</a></sup> that are caused by a substance on the surface, possibly ice or salts, reflecting sunlight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witze2015_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Witze2015-108">[103]</a></sup> A haze periodically appears above Spot 5, the best known bright spot, supporting the hypothesis that some sort of outgassing or sublimating ice formed the bright spots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witze2015_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Witze2015-108">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[104]</a></sup> In March 2016, <i>Dawn</i> found definitive evidence of water molecules on the surface of Ceres at Oxo crater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[106]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 9 December 2015, NASA scientists reported that the bright spots on Ceres may be related to a type of salt, particularly a form of <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a> containing <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_sulfate" title="Magnesium sulfate">magnesium sulfate</a> <a href="/wiki/Sulfate_minerals" title="Sulfate minerals">hexahydrite</a> (MgSO<sub>4</sub>&#183;6H<sub>2</sub>O); the spots were also found to be associated with <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">ammonia-rich clays</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20151209-el_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20151209-el-42">[39]</a></sup> Near-infrared spectra of these bright areas were reported in 2017 to be consistent with a large amount of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_carbonate" title="Sodium carbonate">sodium carbonate</a>, (<span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>) and smaller amounts of ammonium chloride (<span class="chemf nowrap">NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>Cl</span>) or ammonium bicarbonate (<span class="chemf nowrap">NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>HCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[108]</a></sup> These materials have been suggested to originate from the recent crystallization of brines that reached the surface from below.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20160629_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20160629-43">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewin_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lewin-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewin_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lewin-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-De_Sanctis_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-De_Sanctis-45">[42]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon">Carbon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Carbon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">Organic compounds</a> (<a href="/wiki/Tholin" title="Tholin">tholins</a>) were detected on Ceres in Ernutet crater,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PO-20170216_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-PO-20170216-50">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-organics_2018_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-organics_2018-51">[48]</a></sup> and most of the planet\'s surface is extremely rich in carbon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ES-20181227_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-ES-20181227-115">[110]</a></sup> with approximately 20% carbon by mass in its near surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PO_12_2018_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-PO_12_2018-116">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature_12_2018_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nature_12_2018-117">[112]</a></sup> The carbon content is more than five times higher than in <a href="/wiki/Carbonaceous_chondrite" title="Carbonaceous chondrite">carbonaceous chondrite</a> meteorites analyzed on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature_12_2018_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nature_12_2018-117">[112]</a></sup> The surface carbon shows evidence of being mixed with products of rock-water interactions, such as clays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PO_12_2018_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-PO_12_2018-116">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature_12_2018_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nature_12_2018-117">[112]</a></sup> This chemistry suggests Ceres formed in a cold environment, perhaps outside the orbit of Jupiter, and that it accreted from ultra-carbon-rich materials in the presence of water, which could provide conditions favorable to organic chemistry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PO_12_2018_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-PO_12_2018-116">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature_12_2018_117-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nature_12_2018-117">[112]</a></sup> Its presence on Ceres is evidence that the basic ingredients for life can be found throughout the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ES-20181227_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-ES-20181227-115">[110]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:504px;max-width:504px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:">Ceres - Maps of bright areas<br/>&#160;</div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:502px;max-width:502px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA21914-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Map-BrightAreas-20171202.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="989" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="247" width="500"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center">December 2017<br/>&#160;</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:502px;max-width:502px"><div
