Ceres">Bright spots on Ceres</a> in visible and infrared:<br/>"Spot 1" (top row) ("cooler" than surroundings);<br/>"Spot 5" (bottom) ("similar in temperature" as surroundings) (April 2015)</center>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:428px;max-width:428px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:212px;max-width:212px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA20350_crop_-_Occator_from_LAMO.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3420" data-file-width="3420" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/PIA20350_crop_-_Occator_from_LAMO.jpg/210px-PIA20350_crop_-_Occator_from_LAMO.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/PIA20350_crop_-_Occator_from_LAMO.jpg/315px-PIA20350_crop_-_Occator_from_LAMO.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/PIA20350_crop_-_Occator_from_LAMO.jpg/420px-PIA20350_crop_-_Occator_from_LAMO.jpg 2x" width="210"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center"><center><a href="/wiki/Bright_spots_on_Ceres" title="Bright spots on Ceres">"Bright Spot 5"</a> in the crater <a href="/wiki/Occator_(crater)" title="Occator (crater)">Occator</a>. Imaged by <i>Dawn</i> from 385&#160;km (239&#160;mi) (<a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)#Mapping_orbits" title="Dawn (spacecraft)">LAMO</a>)</center></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:212px;max-width:212px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA20348_crop_-_Ceres%27_Ahuna_Mons_top_view.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1412" data-file-width="1412" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/PIA20348_crop_-_Ceres%27_Ahuna_Mons_top_view.jpg/210px-PIA20348_crop_-_Ceres%27_Ahuna_Mons_top_view.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/PIA20348_crop_-_Ceres%27_Ahuna_Mons_top_view.jpg/315px-PIA20348_crop_-_Ceres%27_Ahuna_Mons_top_view.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/PIA20348_crop_-_Ceres%27_Ahuna_Mons_top_view.jpg/420px-PIA20348_crop_-_Ceres%27_Ahuna_Mons_top_view.jpg 2x" width="210"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center"><center><a href="/wiki/Ahuna_Mons" title="Ahuna Mons">Ahuna Mons</a> is an estimated 5&#160;km (3&#160;mi) high on its steepest side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PIA20348_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-PIA20348-118">[113]</a></sup> Imaged by <i>Dawn</i> from 385&#160;km (239&#160;mi) in December 2015.</center></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_structure">Internal structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Internal structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA22660-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Inside-ArtistConcept-20180814.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/PIA22660-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Inside-ArtistConcept-20180814.jpg/150px-PIA22660-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Inside-ArtistConcept-20180814.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/PIA22660-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Inside-ArtistConcept-20180814.jpg/225px-PIA22660-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Inside-ArtistConcept-20180814.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/PIA22660-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Inside-ArtistConcept-20180814.jpg/300px-PIA22660-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Inside-ArtistConcept-20180814.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA22660-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Inside-ArtistConcept-20180814.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Internal structure of Ceres (August 2018)</div></div></div>\n<p>Ceres\' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oblateness" title="Oblateness">oblateness</a> is consistent with a differentiated body, a rocky core overlain with an icy <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2005_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas2005-92">[87]</a></sup> This 100-kilometer-thick mantle (23%&#8211;28% of Ceres by mass; 50% by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volume_(unit)" title="Volume (unit)">volume</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[114]</a></sup> contains up to 200&#160;million cubic kilometers of water, which would be more than the amount of <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a> on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carey2006_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carey2006-120">[115]</a></sup> This result is supported by the observations made by the Keck telescope in 2002 and by evolutionary modeling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCord2005-jgrp_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-McCord2005-jgrp-27">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carry2008_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-Carry2008-96">[91]</a></sup> Also, some characteristics of its surface and history (such as its distance from the Sun, which weakened solar radiation enough to allow some fairly low-freezing-point components to be incorporated during its formation), point to the presence of <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">volatile materials</a> in the interior of Ceres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carry2008_96-3"><a href="#cite_note-Carry2008-96">[91]</a></sup> It has been suggested that a remnant layer of liquid water may have survived to the present under a layer of ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCord2005-jgrp_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-McCord2005-jgrp-27">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O\'Brien2015_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-O\'Brien2015-28">[25]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA22083-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-GravityMapping-20171026.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="788" data-file-width="1400" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/PIA22083-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-GravityMapping-20171026.gif/220px-PIA22083-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-GravityMapping-20171026.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/PIA22083-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-GravityMapping-20171026.gif/330px-PIA22083-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-GravityMapping-20171026.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/PIA22083-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-GravityMapping-20171026.gif/440px-PIA22083-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-GravityMapping-20171026.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA22083-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-GravityMapping-20171026.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Cererian gravity fields: red is high; blue, low.</div></div></div>\n<p>Shape and gravity field measurements by <i>Dawn</i> confirm Ceres is a  body in <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">hydrostatic equilibrium</a> with partial <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">differentiation</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla1112_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla1112-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dawn-news-2016-08-03_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-dawn-news-2016-08-03-121">[116]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Isostasy" title="Isostasy">isostatic compensation</a>, with a mean <a href="/wiki/Moment_of_inertia_factor" title="Moment of inertia factor">moment of inertia</a> of 0.37 (which is similar to that of <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a> at ~0.36).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Park2016_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Park2016-10">[9]</a></sup> The densities of the core and outer layer are estimated to be 2.46&#8211;2.90 and 1.68&#8211;1.95 g/cm<sup>3</sup>, with the latter being about 70&#8211;190&#160;km thick. Only partial dehydration of the core is expected. The high density of the outer layer (relative to water ice) reflects its enrichment in silicates and salts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Park2016_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-Park2016-10">[9]</a></sup> Ceres is the smallest object confirmed to be in hydrostatic equilibrium, being 600&#160;km smaller and less than half the mass of Saturn\'s moon <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">Rhea</a>, the next smallest such object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2010_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas2010-122">[117]</a></sup> Modeling has suggested Ceres could have a small metallic core from partial differentiation of its rocky fraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neumann2015_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neumann2015-123">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bhatia2017_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bhatia2017-124">[119]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere">Atmosphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Atmosphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>There are indications that Ceres has a tenuous water vapor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_body_atmosphere" title="Celestial body atmosphere">atmosphere</a> outgassing from water ice on the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ahearn1992_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ahearn1992-125">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[122]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Surface water ice is unstable at distances less than 5 AU from the Sun,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt2007_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jewitt2007-128">[123]</a></sup> so it is expected to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sublimation_(chemistry)" title="Sublimation (chemistry)">sublime</a> if it is exposed directly to solar radiation. Water ice can migrate from the deep layers of Ceres to the surface, but escapes in a very short time. As a result, it is difficult to detect water vaporization. Water escaping from polar regions of Ceres was possibly observed in the early 1990s but this has not been unambiguously demonstrated. It may be possible to detect escaping water from the surroundings of a fresh impact crater or from cracks in the subsurface layers of Ceres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carry2008_96-4"><a href="#cite_note-Carry2008-96">[91]</a></sup> Ultraviolet observations by the <a href="/wiki/International_Ultraviolet_Explorer" title="International Ultraviolet Explorer">IUE</a> spacecraft detected statistically significant amounts of <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxide</a> ions near Ceres\' north pole, which is a product of water vapor dissociation by ultraviolet solar radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ahearn1992_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ahearn1992-125">[120]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In early 2014, using data from the <i><a href="/wiki/Herschel_Space_Observatory" title="Herschel Space Observatory">Herschel Space Observatory</a></i>, it was discovered that there are several localized (not more than 60&#160;km in diameter) mid-latitude sources of water vapor on Ceres, which each give off approximately <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7026100000000000000&#9824;</span>10<sup>26</sup></span> molecules (or 3&#160;kg) of water per second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuppers2014_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuppers2014-129">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campins2014_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campins2014-130">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[f]</a></sup> Two potential source regions, designated Piazzi (123&#176;E, 21&#176;N) and Region A (231&#176;E, 23&#176;N), have been visualized in the near infrared as dark areas (Region A also has a bright center) by the <a href="/wiki/W._M._Keck_Observatory" title="W. M. Keck Observatory">W. M. Keck Observatory</a>. Possible mechanisms for the vapor release are sublimation from approximately 0.6&#160;km<sup>2</sup> of exposed surface ice, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryovolcanic" title="Cryovolcanic">cryovolcanic</a> eruptions resulting from <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radiogenic</a> internal heat<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuppers2014_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kuppers2014-129">[124]</a></sup> or from pressurization of a subsurface ocean due to growth of an overlying layer of ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O\'Brien2015_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-O\'Brien2015-28">[25]</a></sup> Surface sublimation would be expected to be lower when Ceres is farther from the Sun in its orbit, whereas internally powered emissions should not be affected by its orbital position. The limited data available was more consistent with cometary-style sublimation;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuppers2014_129-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kuppers2014-129">[124]</a></sup> however, subsequent evidence from Dawn strongly suggests ongoing geologic activity could be at least partially responsible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[129]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Studies using <i>Dawn\'s</i> gamma ray and neutron detector (GRaND) reveal that Ceres is accelerating electrons from the solar wind regularly; although there are several possibilities as to what is causing this, the most accepted is that these electrons are being accelerated by collisions between the solar wind and a tenuous water vapor exosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[130]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2017, <i>Dawn</i> confirmed that Ceres has a transient atmosphere that appears to be linked to solar activity. Ice on Ceres can sublimate when energetic particles from the Sun hit exposed ice within craters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[131]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_and_evolution">Origin and evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Origin and evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Ceres is possibly a surviving <a href="/wiki/Protoplanet" title="Protoplanet">protoplanet</a> (planetary embryo), which formed 4.57&#160;billion years ago in the asteroid belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCord2005-jgrp_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-McCord2005-jgrp-27">[24]</a></sup> Although the majority of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_Solar_System" title="Inner Solar System">inner Solar System</a> protoplanets (including all lunar- to Mars-sized bodies) either merged with other protoplanets to form <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a> or were ejected from the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> by Jupiter,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Petit2001_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Petit2001-138">[132]</a></sup> Ceres is thought to have survived relatively intact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCord2005-jgrp_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-McCord2005-jgrp-27">[24]</a></sup> An alternative theory proposes that Ceres formed in the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> and later migrated to the asteroid belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[133]</a></sup> The discovery of ammonia salts in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occator_crater" title="Occator crater">Occator crater</a> supports an origin in the outer Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[134]</a></sup> Another possible protoplanet, Vesta, is less than half the size of Ceres; it suffered a major impact after solidifying, losing ~1% of its mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas1997_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas1997-141">[135]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The geological evolution of Ceres was dependent on the heat sources available during and after its formation: friction from <a href="/wiki/Planetesimal" title="Planetesimal">planetesimal</a> <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a>, and decay of various <a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">radionuclides</a> (possibly including short-lived <a href="/wiki/Extinct_radionuclide" title="Extinct radionuclide">extinct radionuclides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Aluminium-26" title="Aluminium-26">aluminium-26</a>). These are thought to have been sufficient to allow Ceres to differentiate into a rocky <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core_(geology)" title="Core (geology)">core</a> and icy mantle soon after its formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li2006_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-Li2006-15">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCord2005-jgrp_27-5"><a href="#cite_note-McCord2005-jgrp-27">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bhatia2017_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bhatia2017-124">[119]</a></sup> This process may have caused resurfacing by water <a href="/wiki/Volcanism" title="Volcanism">volcanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tectonics" title="Tectonics">tectonics</a>, erasing older geological features.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCord2005-jgrp_27-6"><a href="#cite_note-McCord2005-jgrp-27">[24]</a></sup> Ceres\'s relatively warm surface temperature implies that any of the resulting ice on its surface would have gradually sublimated, leaving behind various hydrated minerals like clay minerals and carbonates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rivkin2006_16-5"><a href="#cite_note-Rivkin2006-16">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Today, Ceres has become considerably less geologically active, with a surface sculpted chiefly by <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">impacts</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li2006_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-Li2006-15">[13]</a></sup> nevertheless, evidence from <i>Dawn</i> reveals that internal processes have continued to sculpt Ceres\'s surface to a significant extent, in stark contrast to Vesta<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[136]</a></sup> and of previous expectations that Ceres would have become geologically dead early in its history due to its small size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCord2005-jgrp_27-7"><a href="#cite_note-McCord2005-jgrp-27">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castillo-Rogez2007_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Castillo-Rogez2007-143">[137]</a></sup> The presence of significant amounts of water ice in its crust<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2005_92-3"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas2005-92">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-organics_2018_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-organics_2018-51">[48]</a></sup> and evidence of recent geological resurfacing, raises the possibility that Ceres has a layer of liquid water in its interior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCord2005-jgrp_27-8"><a href="#cite_note-McCord2005-jgrp-27">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castillo-Rogez2007_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-Castillo-Rogez2007-143">[137]</a></sup> This hypothetical layer is often called an ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rivkin2006_16-6"><a href="#cite_note-Rivkin2006-16">[14]</a></sup> If such a layer of liquid water exists, it is hypothesized to be located between the rocky core and ice mantle like that of the theorized ocean on <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCord2005-jgrp_27-9"><a href="#cite_note-McCord2005-jgrp-27">[24]</a></sup> The existence of an ocean is more likely if <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solutes" title="Solutes">solutes</a> (i.e. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salts" title="Salts">salts</a>), <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> or other <a href="/wiki/Antifreeze" title="Antifreeze">antifreeze</a> compounds are dissolved in the water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCord2005-jgrp_27-10"><a href="#cite_note-McCord2005-jgrp-27">[24]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_habitability">Potential habitability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Potential habitability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Although not as actively discussed as a <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">potential home</a> for <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microbial</a> <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a> as <a href="/wiki/Life_on_Mars" title="Life on Mars">Mars</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_on_Europa" title="Life on Europa">Europa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enceladus#Potential_habitability" title="Enceladus">Enceladus</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Life_on_Titan" title="Life on Titan">Titan</a>, there is evidence that Ceres\' icy mantle was once a watery subterranean ocean, and that has led to speculations that life could have existed there,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[141]</a></sup> and that hypothesized <a href="/wiki/Ejecta" title="Ejecta">ejecta</a> bearing microorganisms could have come from Ceres to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[143]</a></sup> The remote detection of <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> and the presence of water with 20% carbon by mass in its near surface, could provide conditions favorable to organic chemistry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PO_12_2018_116-3"><a href="#cite_note-PO_12_2018-116">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature_12_2018_117-4"><a href="#cite_note-Nature_12_2018-117">[112]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation_and_exploration">Observation and exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Observation and exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation">Observation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Observation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ceres_Polarmetric_Map.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="861" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Ceres_Polarmetric_Map.jpg/220px-Ceres_Polarmetric_Map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Ceres_Polarmetric_Map.jpg/330px-Ceres_Polarmetric_Map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Ceres_Polarmetric_Map.jpg/440px-Ceres_Polarmetric_Map.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ceres_Polarmetric_Map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Polarimetry" title="Polarimetry">Polarimetric</a> map of Ceres<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[144]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>When in <a href="/wiki/Opposition_(planets)" title="Opposition (planets)">opposition</a> near its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a>, Ceres can reach an <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> of +6.7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pasachoff1983_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pasachoff1983-17">[15]</a></sup> This is generally regarded as too dim to be visible to the <a href="/wiki/Naked_eye" title="Naked eye">naked eye</a>, but under ideal viewing conditions, keen eyes with <a href="/wiki/Visual_acuity" title="Visual acuity">20/20 vision</a> may be able to see it. The only other asteroids that can reach a similarly bright magnitude are <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta#Visibility" title="4 Vesta">4 Vesta</a> and, when in rare oppositions near their perihelions, <a href="/wiki/2_Pallas#Characteristics" title="2 Pallas">2 Pallas</a> and <a href="/wiki/7_Iris" title="7 Iris">7 Iris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[145]</a></sup> When in <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Conjunction_(opposition)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Conjunction (opposition) (page does not exist)">conjunction</a>, Ceres has a magnitude of around +9.3, which corresponds to the faintest objects visible with 10&#215;50 <a href="/wiki/Binoculars" title="Binoculars">binoculars</a>; thus it can be seen with such binoculars in a <a href="/wiki/Light_pollution" title="Light pollution">naturally dark</a> and clear <a href="/wiki/Night_sky" title="Night sky">night sky</a> around <a href="/wiki/New_moon" title="New moon">new moon</a>.\n</p><p>Some notable observations and milestones for Ceres include the following:\n</p>\n<ul><li>1984 November 13: An <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occultation</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">star</a> by Ceres observed in <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, Florida and across the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Millis1987_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Millis1987-152">[146]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1995 June 25: <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">Ultraviolet</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a></i> images with 50-kilometer resolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parker2002_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-Parker2002-94">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[147]</a></sup></li>\n<li>2002: <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">Infrared</a> images with 30-km resolution taken with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keck_telescope" title="Keck telescope">Keck telescope</a> using <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_optics" title="Adaptive optics">adaptive optics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keck_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-Keck-97">[92]</a></sup></li>\n<li>2003 and 2004: Visible light images with 30-km resolution (the best prior to the <i>Dawn</i> mission) taken using <i>Hubble</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li2006_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-Li2006-15">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hubbl12003-4_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hubbl12003-4-98">[93]</a></sup></li>\n<li>2012 December 22: Ceres <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occulted</a> the star TYC 1865-00446-1 over parts of Japan, Russia, and China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asteroid_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-asteroid-154">[148]</a></sup> Ceres\' brightness was magnitude 6.9 and the star, 12.2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asteroid_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-asteroid-154">[148]</a></sup></li>\n<li>2014: Ceres was found to have an atmosphere with water vapor, confirmed by the <a href="/wiki/Herschel_Space_Observatory" title="Herschel Space Observatory"><i>Herschel</i> space telescope</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[149]</a></sup></li>\n<li>2015: The NASA <a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)"><i>Dawn</i> spacecraft</a> approached and orbited Ceres, sending detailed images and scientific data back to Earth.</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1147" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg/200px-Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg/300px-Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg/400px-Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dawn_Flight_Configuration_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist\'s conception of <a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)"><i>Dawn</i></a>, travelling from Vesta to Ceres</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1981, a proposal for an asteroid mission was submitted to the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> (ESA). Named the Asteroidal Gravity Optical and Radar Analysis (AGORA), this <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a> was to launch some time in 1990&#8211;1994 and perform two flybys of large asteroids. The preferred target for this mission was Vesta. AGORA would reach the asteroid belt either by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_slingshot" title="Gravitational slingshot">gravitational slingshot</a> trajectory past Mars or by means of a small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_engine" title="Ion engine">ion engine</a>. However, the proposal was refused by ESA. A joint <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>&#8211;ESA asteroid mission was then drawn up for a Multiple Asteroid Orbiter with Solar Electric Propulsion (MAOSEP), with one of the mission profiles including an orbit of Vesta. NASA indicated they were not interested in an asteroid mission. Instead, ESA set up a technological study of a spacecraft with an ion drive. Other missions to the asteroid belt were proposed in the 1980s by France, Germany, Italy, and the United States, but none were approved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ulivi_harland08_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-ulivi_harland08-156">[150]</a></sup> Exploration of Ceres by fly-by and impacting penetrator was the second main target of the second plan of the multiaimed Soviet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vesta_mission" title="Vesta mission">Vesta mission</a>, developed in cooperation with European countries for realisation in 1991&#8211;1994 but canceled due to the Soviet Union disbanding.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA17937-MarsCuriosityRover-FirstAsteroidImage-20140420.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="576" data-file-width="768" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/PIA17937-MarsCuriosityRover-FirstAsteroidImage-20140420.jpg/200px-PIA17937-MarsCuriosityRover-FirstAsteroidImage-20140420.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/PIA17937-MarsCuriosityRover-FirstAsteroidImage-20140420.jpg/300px-PIA17937-MarsCuriosityRover-FirstAsteroidImage-20140420.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/PIA17937-MarsCuriosityRover-FirstAsteroidImage-20140420.jpg/400px-PIA17937-MarsCuriosityRover-FirstAsteroidImage-20140420.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA17937-MarsCuriosityRover-FirstAsteroidImage-20140420.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> image (Ceres and Vesta) from <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> &#8211; viewed by <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)"><i>Curiosity</i></a> (20 April 2014)</div></div></div>\n<p>In the early 1990s, NASA initiated the <a href="/wiki/Discovery_Program" title="Discovery Program">Discovery Program</a>, which was intended to be a series of low-cost scientific missions. In 1996, the program\'s study team recommended as a high priority a mission to explore the asteroid belt using a spacecraft with an <a href="/wiki/Ion_thruster" title="Ion thruster">ion engine</a>. Funding for this program remained problematic for several years, but by 2004 the <i><a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)">Dawn</a></i> vehicle had passed its critical design review.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2007_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2007-157">[151]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was launched on 27 September 2007, as the space mission to make the first visits to both Vesta and Ceres. On 3 May 2011, <i>Dawn</i> acquired its first targeting image 1.2 million kilometers from Vesta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pr2011-138_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-pr2011-138-158">[152]</a></sup> After orbiting Vesta for 13 months, <i>Dawn</i> used its ion engine to depart for Ceres, with gravitational capture occurring on 6 March 2015<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schenk2015-01-15_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schenk2015-01-15-159">[153]</a></sup> at a separation of 61,000&#160;km,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rayman2014.12.01_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rayman2014.12.01-160">[154]</a></sup> four months prior to the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i> flyby of Pluto.\n</p><p><i>Dawn\'s</i> mission profile called for it to study Ceres from a series of circular polar orbits at successively lower altitudes. It entered its first observational orbit ("RC3") around Ceres at an altitude of 13,500&#160;km on 23 April 2015, staying for only approximately one orbit (fifteen days).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rayman2015.03.06_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rayman2015.03.06-32">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rayman2014.03.03_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rayman2014.03.03-161">[155]</a></sup> The spacecraft subsequently reduced its orbital distance to 4,400&#160;km for its second observational orbit ("survey") for three weeks,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rayman2014.05.07_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rayman2014.05.07-162">[156]</a></sup> then down to 1,470&#160;km ("HAMO;" high altitude mapping orbit) for two months<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rayman2014.07.01_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rayman2014.07.01-163">[157]</a></sup> and then down to its final orbit at 375&#160;km ("LAMO;" low altitude mapping orbit) for at least three months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rayman2014.09.02_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rayman2014.09.02-164">[158]</a></sup> The spacecraft instrumentation includes a framing camera, a visual and infrared <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">spectrometer</a>, and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma-ray" title="Gamma-ray">gamma-ray</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> detector. These instruments examined Ceres\' shape and elemental composition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russel2006_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russel2006-165">[159]</a></sup> On 13 January 2015, <i>Dawn</i> took the first images of Ceres at near-<i>Hubble</i> resolution, revealing impact craters and a small high-albedo spot on the surface, near the same location as that observed previously. Additional imaging sessions, at increasingly better resolution took place on 25 January, 4, 12, 19, and 25 February, 1 March, and 10 and 15 April.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rayman2015.01.30_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rayman2015.01.30-166">[160]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Dawn</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> arrival in a stable orbit around Ceres was delayed after, close to reaching Ceres, it was hit by a <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic ray</a>, making it take another, longer route around Ceres in back, instead of a direct spiral towards it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SafeMode2014_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-SafeMode2014-167">[161]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/China_National_Space_Administration" title="China National Space Administration">Chinese Space Agency</a> is designing a <a href="/wiki/Sample-return_mission" title="Sample-return mission">sample-return mission</a> from Ceres that would take place during the 2020s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[162]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Maps">Maps</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Maps">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="wikitable" tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:704px;max-width:704px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">Ceres &#8211; Survey Maps (June 2015)</div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:702px;max-width:702px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" quadrangles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Map of quadrangles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The following <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imagemap" title="Imagemap">imagemap</a> of the dwarf planet Ceres is divided into 15 <a href="/wiki/Quadrangle_(geography)" title="Quadrangle (geography)">quadrangles</a>. They are named after the first craters whose <a href="/wiki/List_of_geological_features_on_Ceres" title="List of geological features on Ceres">names</a> the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">IAU</a> approved in July 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS-20150713_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-20150713-170">[164]</a></sup> The map image(s) were taken by the <a href="/wiki/Dawn_(spacecraft)" title="Dawn (spacecraft)"><i>Dawn</i></a> space probe.\n</p>\n<div class="center"><div class="noresize thumb tnone" style=";">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="overflow:hidden;width:622px;">\n<div class="thumbimage" style="height:310px; overflow:hidden; position:relative; background-color:white;">\n<div style=";left:0px; top:0px; width:620px; position:absolute;"> <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20150319-Ceres-DwarfPlanet-Dawn-OpNav5HIPASS-QuadNames-blank.png" title="Topographic map of Ceres as of February 2015. Darker areas represent lower elevations, and brighter areas represent higher elevations. class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This image was taken by the <i>Dawn</i> spacecraft on 2 May 2015, during a "rotation characterization" orbit, 13,642 kilometres (8,477&#160;mi) above the surface of Ceres. Visible at center and center right are two <a href="/wiki/Bright_spots_on_Ceres" title="Bright spots on Ceres">bright spots</a>, a phenomenon common on Ceres, in Oxo and <a href="/wiki/Haulani_(crater)" title="Haulani (crater)">Haulani</a> craters respectively. <a href="/wiki/Ahuna_Mons" title="Ahuna Mons">Ahuna Mons</a> is also visible in the image as a noticeable, bluff hill, seen just right of bottom.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The value of 0.37 is the mean moment of inertia, which is thought to better represent Ceres\' interior structure than the polar moment of inertia (0.39), due to the high polar flattening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Park2016_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Park2016-10">[9]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Due to its <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric orbit</a>, the dwarf planet <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> was also within the orbit of Neptune from 1979 to 1999, and will be again from approximately 2227 to 2247.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-61">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">All other languages but one use a variant of <i>Ceres/Cerere</i>: Russian <i>Tserera</i>, Persian <i>Seres</i>, Japanese <i>Keresu</i>. The exception is Chinese, which uses \'grain-god(dess) star\' (&#31296;&#31070;&#26143; <i>g&#468;sh&#233;nx&#299;ng</i>). Note that this is unlike the goddess Ceres, where Chinese does use the Latin name (&#21051;&#29790;&#26031; <i>k&#232;ru&#236;s&#299;</i>).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-67">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1807 Klaproth tried to change the name to the more etymologically justified "cererium", but it did not catch on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[62]</a></sup> </span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-133"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-133">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> This emission rate is modest compared to those calculated for the tidally driven plumes of <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">Enceladus</a> (a smaller body) and <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a> (a larger body), 200&#160;kg/s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen2006_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen2006-131">[126]</a></sup> and 7000&#160;kg/s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Europa_tidal_forces_2013_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Europa_tidal_forces_2013-132">[127]</a></sup> respectively.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ceres_(dwarf_planet)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Lutz_D._Schmadel" title="Lutz D.
